data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="text-align:right; padding-right:0.5em;">Induction</th><td style="padding-left:0.5em;">\n<a href="/wiki/Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_balloting,_1969" title="Baseball Hall of Fame balloting, 1969">1969</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="text-align:right; padding-right:0.5em;">Vote</th><td style="padding-left:0.5em;">\n93.2% (first ballot)</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">\n</th></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Stanley Frank Musial</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/ju\xcb\x90/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;cute&#39;">ju\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span></span>, <wbr />-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xca\x92/: &#39;s&#39; in &#39;pleasure&#39;">\xca\x92</span><span title="/\xc9\x99l/: &#39;le&#39; in &#39;bottle&#39;">\xc9\x99l</span></span>/</a></span></span>; born <b>Stanis\xc5\x82aw Franciszek Musia\xc5\x82</b>; November 21, 1920&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 January 19, 2013), nicknamed <b>Stan the Man</b>, was an American <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a> <a href="/wiki/Outfielder" title="Outfielder">outfielder</a> and <a href="/wiki/First_baseman" title="First baseman">first baseman</a>. He spent 22 seasons in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> (MLB) playing for the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Cardinals</a>, from 1941 to 1944 and 1946 to 1963.  Widely considered to be one of the greatest and most consistent hitters in baseball history,<sup id="cite_ref-Stan_Musial:_An_American_Life_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stan_Musial:_An_American_Life-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Musial was a first-ballot inductee into the <a href="/wiki/National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum" title="National Baseball Hall of Fame and Museum">Baseball Hall of Fame</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_balloting,_1969" title="Baseball Hall of Fame balloting, 1969">1969</a>, and was also selected to the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals_Hall_of_Fame_Museum" title="St. Louis Cardinals Hall of Fame Museum">St. Louis Cardinals Hall of Fame</a> in the inaugural class of 2014.\n</p><p>Musial <a href="/wiki/Batting_average" title="Batting average">batted</a> .331 over the course of his career and set <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> (NL) records for career <a href="/wiki/Hit_(baseball)" title="Hit (baseball)">hits</a> (3,630), <a href="/wiki/Run_batted_in" title="Run batted in">runs batted in</a> (1,951), games played (3,026), <a href="/wiki/At_bat" title="At bat">at bats</a> (10,972), <a href="/wiki/Run_(baseball)" title="Run (baseball)">runs scored</a> (1,949) and <a href="/wiki/Double_(baseball)" title="Double (baseball)">doubles</a> (725). His 475 career <a href="/wiki/Home_run" title="Home run">home runs</a> then ranked second in NL history behind <a href="/wiki/Mel_Ott" title="Mel Ott">Mel Ott</a>\'s total of 511. His 6,134 <a href="/wiki/Total_bases" title="Total bases">total bases</a> remained a major league record until surpassed by <a href="/wiki/Hank_Aaron" title="Hank Aaron">Hank Aaron</a>, and his hit total still ranks <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_career_hits_leaders" title="List of Major League Baseball career hits leaders">fourth all-time</a>, and is the highest by any player who spent his career with only one team. A seven-time batting champion with identical totals of 1,815 hits at home and 1,815 hits on the road, he was named the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a>\'s (NL) <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Major League Baseball Most Valuable Player Award">Most Valuable Player</a> (MVP) three times and led St. Louis to three <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> championships. He also shares the major league record for the most <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="Major League Baseball All-Star Game">All-Star Games</a> played (24) with <a href="/wiki/Hank_Aaron" title="Hank Aaron">Hank Aaron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Willie_Mays" title="Willie Mays">Willie Mays</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Musial was born in <a href="/wiki/Donora,_Pennsylvania" title="Donora, Pennsylvania">Donora, Pennsylvania</a>, where he frequently played baseball informally or in organized settings, and eventually played on the baseball team at <a href="/wiki/Ringgold_High_School_(Pennsylvania)" title="Ringgold High School (Pennsylvania)">Donora High School</a>. Signed to a professional contract by the St. Louis Cardinals as a <a href="/wiki/Pitcher" title="Pitcher">pitcher</a> in 1938, Musial was converted into an outfielder prior to his major league debut in 1941. Noted for his unique batting stance, he quickly established himself as a consistent and productive hitter. In his first full season, 1942, the Cardinals won the <a href="/wiki/1942_World_Series" title="1942 World Series">World Series</a>. The following year, he led the NL in six different offensive categories and earned his first MVP award. He was also named to the NL All-Star squad for the first time; he appeared in every All-Star game in every subsequent season he played.<sup id="cite_ref-sportsdatallc.com_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sportsdatallc.com-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Musial won his second World Series championship in <a href="/wiki/1944_World_Series" title="1944 World Series">1944</a>, then missed the entire 1945 season while serving in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">Navy</a>.\n</p><p>After completing his military service during the war, Musial returned to baseball in 1946 and resumed his consistent hitting. That year he earned his second MVP award and third World Series title. His third MVP award came in 1948, when he finished one home run short of winning baseball\'s <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Triple_Crown" class="mw-redirect" title="Major League Baseball Triple Crown">Triple Crown</a>. After struggling offensively in 1959, Musial used a personal trainer to help maintain his productivity until he decided to retire in 1963. At the time of his retirement, he held or shared 17 major league records, 29 National League records, and nine All-Star Game records. Ironically, in 1964, the season following his retirement, the Cardinals went on to defeat the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a> in an epic 7-game clash, for St. Louis\' first <a href="/wiki/1964_World_Series" title="1964 World Series">World Series</a> championship in nearly two decades (a team which included future Hall of Famer <a href="/wiki/Lou_Brock" title="Lou Brock">Lou Brock</a> performing what would have likely been Musial\'s left field duties).<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In addition to overseeing businesses, such as a restaurant both before and after his playing career, Musial served as the Cardinals\' <a href="/wiki/General_manager_(baseball)" title="General manager (baseball)">general manager</a> in 1967, winning the pennant and <a href="/wiki/1967_World_Series" title="1967 World Series">World Series</a>, then quitting that position. He also became noted for his harmonica playing, a skill he acquired during his playing career. Known for his modesty and sportsmanship, Musial was selected for the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_All-Century_Team" title="Major League Baseball All-Century Team">Major League Baseball All-Century Team</a> in 1999. In February 2011, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> presented Musial with the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a>, one of the highest <a href="/wiki/Awards_and_decorations_of_the_United_States_government" title="Awards and decorations of the United States government">civilian awards</a> that can be bestowed on a person by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_government" class="mw-redirect" title="United States government">United States government</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Early_life"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Early life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Professional_baseball_career"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Professional baseball career</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Minor_leagues_(1938\xe2\x80\x9341)"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Minor leagues (1938\xe2\x80\x9341)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Major_leagues_(1941\xe2\x80\x9344)"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Major leagues (1941\xe2\x80\x9344)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Sojourn_in_the_U.S._Navy_(1945\xe2\x80\x9346)"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Sojourn in the U.S. Navy (1945\xe2\x80\x9346)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Major_leagues_(1946\xe2\x80\x9363)"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Major leagues (1946\xe2\x80\x9363)</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-7"><a href="#1946\xe2\x80\x9349"><span class="tocnumber">2.4.1</span> <span class="toctext">1946\xe2\x80\x9349</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-8"><a href="#1950\xe2\x80\x9354"><span class="tocnumber">2.4.2</span> <span class="toctext">1950\xe2\x80\x9354</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-9"><a href="#1955\xe2\x80\x9359"><span class="tocnumber">2.4.3</span> <span class="toctext">1955\xe2\x80\x9359</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-10"><a href="#1960\xe2\x80\x9363"><span class="tocnumber">2.4.4</span> <span class="toctext">1960\xe2\x80\x9363</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Post-playing_career_and_family_life"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Post-playing career and family life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Honors_and_recognition"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Honors and recognition</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#MLB_statistics"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">MLB statistics</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Hitting"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Hitting</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Fielding"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Fielding</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Value"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Value</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stan_Musial&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Musial was born in <a href="/wiki/Donora,_Pennsylvania" title="Donora, Pennsylvania">Donora, Pennsylvania</a>, the fifth of the six children (four girls and two boys) of Lukasz and Mary (Lancos) Musia\xc5\x82 (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/u\xcb\x90/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">u\xcb\x90</span><span title="/\xca\x83/: &#39;sh&#39; in &#39;shy&#39;">\xca\x83</span><span title="/a\xca\x8a/: &#39;ou&#39; in &#39;mouth&#39;">a\xca\x8a</span></span>/</a></span></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-Musial6_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Musial6-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> His mother was of <a href="/wiki/Rusyns" title="Rusyns">Carpatho-Rusyn</a> descent and his father was a <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Polish</a> immigrant who chose the name Stanis\xc5\x82aw Franciszek for his first son, though his father always referred to Musial using the Polish nickname Stasiu, pronounced "Stashu".<sup id="cite_ref-Musial6_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Musial6-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Musial frequently played baseball with his brother Ed and other friends during his childhood, and considered <a href="/wiki/Lefty_Grove" title="Lefty Grove">Lefty Grove</a> his favorite ballplayer.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Musial also had the benefit of learning about baseball from his neighbor Joe Barbao, a former <a href="/wiki/Minor_League_Baseball" title="Minor League Baseball">minor league</a> pitcher.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> When he enrolled in school, his name was formally changed to Stanley Frank Musial.<sup id="cite_ref-Musial6_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Musial6-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At age 15 Musial joined the Donora Zincs, a semi-professional team managed by Barbao.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche7_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche7-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In his Zincs debut he pitched 6 <a href="/wiki/Inning" title="Inning">innings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strikeout" title="Strikeout">struck out</a> 13 batters, all of them adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche7_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche7-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Musial also played one season on the newly revived Donora High School baseball team, where one of his teammates was Buddy Griffey, father of MLB player <a href="/wiki/Ken_Griffey_Sr." title="Ken Griffey Sr.">Ken Griffey Sr.</a> and grandfather to <a href="/wiki/Ken_Griffey_Jr." title="Ken Griffey Jr.">Ken Griffey Jr.</a><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Baseball statistician <a href="/wiki/Bill_James" title="Bill James">Bill James</a> described the younger Griffey, in comparison to Musial, as "the second-best left-handed hitting, left-handed throwing outfielder ever born in Donora, Pennsylvania, on November 21."<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> His exploits as a rising player in Pennsylvania earned him the nickname "The Donora Greyhound".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Musial also played basketball and was offered an athletic scholarship in that sport by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">University of Pittsburgh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche7_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche7-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Cardinals</a> had scouted Musial as a pitcher and, in 1937, offered him a professional contract after a workout with their <a href="/wiki/Minor_league_baseball" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor league baseball">Class D</a> Penn State League affiliate.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Musial\'s father initially resisted the idea of his son pursuing a baseball career, but reluctantly gave his consent after lobbying by both Musial and his mother.<sup id="cite_ref-Giglio2627_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giglio2627-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>  Musial also credited his school librarian Helen Kloz for pointing out that baseball was his dream and advising him to pursue it professionally.<sup id="cite_ref-Giglio2627_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giglio2627-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In what was then a common practice, the Cardinals did not file the contract with the <a href="/wiki/Commissioner_of_Baseball" title="Commissioner of Baseball">baseball commissioner</a>\'s office until June 1938. This preserved Musial\'s amateur eligibility, and he was still able to participate in high school sports, leading Donora High School\'s basketball team to a playoff appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He then reported to the Cardinals\' Class D affiliate in <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Williamson_Red_Birds" title="Williamson Red Birds">Williamson Red Birds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_baseball_career">Professional baseball career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stan_Musial&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Professional baseball career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Minor_leagues_.281938.E2.80.9341.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_leagues_(1938\xe2\x80\x9341)">Minor leagues (1938\xe2\x80\x9341)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stan_Musial&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Minor leagues (1938\xe2\x80\x9341)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Musial\'s rookie year with Williamson in 1938 was a period of adjustment both on and off the field. He began gaining more in-depth knowledge about baseball strategy<sup id="cite_ref-Musial2728_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Musial2728-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> while posting a 6\xe2\x80\x936 <a href="/wiki/Win%E2%80%93loss_record_(pitching)" title="Win\xe2\x80\x93loss record (pitching)">win\xe2\x80\x93loss record</a> and a 4.66 <a href="/wiki/Earned_run_average" title="Earned run average">earned run average</a> (ERA), to go along with a .258 <a href="/wiki/Batting_average" title="Batting average">batting average</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Off the field, he experienced feelings of <a href="/wiki/Homesickness" title="Homesickness">homesickness</a> while learning to live comfortably and independently on his $65-per-month salary.<sup id="cite_ref-Musial2728_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Musial2728-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Musial finished his high school education before returning to Williamson in spring 1939. That season his numbers improved to a 9\xe2\x80\x932 record, a 4.30 ERA, and a .352 batting average.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Musial spent the 1940 season with the Cardinals\' other Class D team, the <a href="/wiki/Daytona_Beach_Islanders" title="Daytona Beach Islanders">Daytona Beach Islanders</a>, where he developed a lifelong friendship with manager <a href="/wiki/Dickie_Kerr" class="mw-redirect" title="Dickie Kerr">Dickie Kerr</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> His pitching skills improved under the guidance of Kerr, who also recognized his <a href="/wiki/Hit_(baseball)" title="Hit (baseball)">hitting</a> talent, playing him in the <a href="/wiki/Outfielder" title="Outfielder">outfield</a> between pitching <a href="/wiki/Starting_pitcher" title="Starting pitcher">starts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> On May 25, 1940, Musial married fellow Donora resident, Lillian "Lil" Labash, in Daytona Beach, and the couple\'s first child followed in August.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> During late August, Musial suffered a shoulder injury while playing in the outfield, and later made an early exit as the starting pitcher in a 12\xe2\x80\x935 playoff game loss.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> For a while Musial considered leaving baseball entirely, complaining that he could not afford to support himself and his wife on the $16 a week pay. Kerr talked him out of it, and even took the Musials into his own home to relieve the financial burden. To repay the debt Musial bought Kerr a $20,000 home in Houston in 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In 113 games in 1940 he hit .311, while compiling an 18\xe2\x80\x935 pitching record that included 176 strikeouts and 145 <a href="/wiki/Base_on_balls" title="Base on balls">walks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Musial was assigned to the Class AA <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Red_Birds" title="Columbus Red Birds">Columbus Red Birds</a> to begin 1941, though manager, <a href="/wiki/Burt_Shotton" title="Burt Shotton">Burt Shotton</a>, and Musial himself quickly realized that the previous year\'s injury had considerably weakened his arm.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> He was reassigned to the Class C <a href="/wiki/Springfield_Cardinals" title="Springfield Cardinals">Springfield Cardinals</a> as a full-time outfielder, and he later credited manager <a href="/wiki/Ollie_Vanek" title="Ollie Vanek">Ollie Vanek</a> for displaying confidence in his hitting ability.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> During 87 games with Springfield, Musial hit a league-leading .379 before being promoted to the <a href="/wiki/Rochester_Red_Wings" title="Rochester Red Wings">Rochester Red Wings</a> of the <a href="/wiki/International_League" title="International League">International League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> He was noted for his unique batting stance, a crouch in which his back was seemingly square to the pitcher.<sup id="cite_ref-espnclassic_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-espnclassic-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> This stance was later described by pitcher <a href="/wiki/Ted_Lyons" title="Ted Lyons">Ted Lyons</a> as "a kid peeking around the corner to see if the cops were coming".<sup id="cite_ref-sabr_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sabr-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> According to a 1950 description by author <a href="/wiki/Tom_Meany" title="Tom Meany">Tom Meany</a>, "The bent knees and the crouch give him the appearance of a coiled spring, although most pitchers think of him as a coiled rattlesnake."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Musial continued to play well in Rochester\xe2\x80\x94in one three-game stretch, he had 11 hits. He was called up to the Cardinals for the last two weeks of the 1941 season.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Major_leagues_.281941.E2.80.9344.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_leagues_(1941\xe2\x80\x9344)">Major leagues (1941\xe2\x80\x9344)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stan_Musial&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Major leagues (1941\xe2\x80\x9344)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Musial_statue.JPG" class="image"><img alt="A bronze statue of baseball great Stan Musial" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/dd/Musial_statue.JPG/170px-Musial_statue.JPG" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/dd/Musial_statue.JPG/255px-Musial_statue.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/dd/Musial_statue.JPG 2x" data-file-width="274" data-file-height="364" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Musial_statue.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Musial\'s statue outside of <a href="/wiki/Busch_Stadium" title="Busch Stadium">Busch Stadium</a> captures his signature batting stance.</div></div></div>\n<p>Musial made his <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">major league</a> debut during the second game of a <a href="/wiki/Doubleheader_(baseball)" title="Doubleheader (baseball)">doubleheader</a> at <a href="/wiki/Sportsman%27s_Park" title="Sportsman&#39;s Park">Sportsman\'s Park</a> on September 17, 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The Cardinals were in the midst of a pennant race with the <a href="/wiki/1941_Brooklyn_Dodgers_season" title="1941 Brooklyn Dodgers season">Brooklyn Dodgers</a>; in 12 games, Musial collected 20 hits for a .426 batting average.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Despite Musial\'s late contributions, the <a href="/wiki/1941_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1941 St. Louis Cardinals season">Cardinals</a> finished two and one-half games behind the 100-game-winning Dodgers.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cardinals manager <a href="/wiki/Billy_Southworth" title="Billy Southworth">Billy Southworth</a> used Musial as a <a href="/wiki/Left_fielder" title="Left fielder">left fielder</a> to begin <a href="/wiki/1942_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1942 St. Louis Cardinals season">1942</a>, sometimes lifting him for a <a href="/wiki/Pinch-hitter" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinch-hitter">pinch-hitter</a> against left-handed pitching.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Musial was hitting .315 by late June,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> as the Cardinals resumed battling the Dodgers for first place in the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> (NL).<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The Cardinals took sole possession of first place on September 13, and when Musial caught a fly ball to end the first game of a doubleheader on September 27 they clinched the <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_League_pennant_winners" title="List of National League pennant winners">pennant</a> with their 105th win.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> He finished the season with a .315 batting average and 72 <a href="/wiki/Runs_batted_in" class="mw-redirect" title="Runs batted in">runs batted in</a> (RBI) in 140 games. Musial received national publicity when he was named by <i><a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Post-Dispatch" title="St. Louis Post-Dispatch">St. Louis Post-Dispatch</a></i> sports editor J. Roy Stockton as his choice for Rookie of the Year in a <i><a href="/wiki/Saturday_Evening_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Saturday Evening Post">Saturday Evening Post</a></i> article.<sup id="cite_ref-stats1_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats1-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Cardinals played the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> champion <a href="/wiki/1942_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1942 New York Yankees season">New York Yankees</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1942_World_Series" title="1942 World Series">1942 World Series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Representing the winning run at home plate in the bottom of the ninth inning of Game 1 at Sportsman\'s Park, Musial <a href="/wiki/Ground_out_(baseball)" title="Ground out (baseball)">grounded out</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Bases_loaded" class="mw-redirect" title="Bases loaded">bases loaded</a> to end the game .<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Musial\'s first hit of the Series was an RBI <a href="/wiki/Single_(baseball)" title="Single (baseball)">single</a> that provided the margin of victory in Game 2, allowing the Cardinals to tie the Series.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Over the next three games at <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Stadium_(1923)" title="Yankee Stadium (1923)">Yankee Stadium</a>, Musial had three more hits as the Cardinals defeated the Yankees in the Series four games to one.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Musial batted .222 for the Series, with two <a href="/wiki/Run_(baseball)" title="Run (baseball)">runs scored</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Musial\'s <a href="/wiki/1943_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1943 St. Louis Cardinals season">1943 season</a> started with a brief contract holdout in <a href="/wiki/Spring_training" title="Spring training">spring training</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> He made the National League All-Star team for the first time as a starting left fielder and got a <a href="/wiki/Double_(baseball)" title="Double (baseball)">double</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1943_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="1943 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">All-Star Game</a> on July 13.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> He finished the season leading the major leagues in hitting with a .357 batting average and led the NL in hits (220), <a href="/wiki/Double_(baseball)" title="Double (baseball)">doubles</a> (48), <a href="/wiki/Triple_(baseball)" title="Triple (baseball)">triples</a> (20), <a href="/wiki/Total_bases" title="Total bases">total bases</a> (347), <a href="/wiki/On-base_percentage" title="On-base percentage">on-base percentage</a> (.425), and <a href="/wiki/Slugging_percentage" title="Slugging percentage">slugging percentage</a> (.562).<sup id="cite_ref-Giglio86_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giglio86-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> This performance earned him his first <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Major League Baseball Most Valuable Player Award">NL Most Valuable Player Award</a>, ahead of teammate and catcher <a href="/wiki/Walker_Cooper" title="Walker Cooper">Walker Cooper</a> (.318 batting average).<sup id="cite_ref-Giglio86_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giglio86-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> After romping to another NL pennant by 18 games, the Cardinals again faced the Yankees in the <a href="/wiki/1943_World_Series" title="1943 World Series">1943 World Series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Giglio87_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giglio87-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Musial had a single in the Cardinals\' Game 1 loss, and scored a run in a Game 2 win.<sup id="cite_ref-Giglio87_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giglio87-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The Cardinals did not win another game in the Series, but the loser\'s bonus share paid to each Cardinals player ($4,321.99) still amounted to nearly two-thirds of Musial\'s regular season salary.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>United States involvement in World War II began to impinge on Musial\'s baseball career in <a href="/wiki/1944_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1944 St. Louis Cardinals season">1944</a>, as he underwent a physical examination in prelude to possible service in the armed forces.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> He ultimately remained with the Cardinals for the entire season, posting a .347 batting average with 197 hits.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The Cardinals claimed the NL pennant for the third consecutive season, and faced St. Louis\'s other major league team, the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">Browns</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/1944_World_Series" title="1944 World Series">1944 World Series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The Browns took a 2\xe2\x80\x931 lead, while Musial hit .250 with zero RBI.<sup id="cite_ref-Giglio93_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giglio93-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> He broke out in Game 4 with a two-run <a href="/wiki/Home_run" title="Home run">home run</a>, single, double, and a walk as part of a 5\xe2\x80\x931 Cardinals win.<sup id="cite_ref-Giglio93_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giglio93-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The Cardinals went on to defeat the Browns in six games, and Musial posted a .304 batting average for the Series.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Sojourn_in_the_U.S._Navy_.281945.E2.80.9346.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sojourn_in_the_U.S._Navy_(1945\xe2\x80\x9346)">Sojourn in the U.S. Navy (1945\xe2\x80\x9346)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stan_Musial&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sojourn in the U.S. Navy (1945\xe2\x80\x9346)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Musial enlisted in the United States Navy on January 23, 1945, during World War II. He was initially assigned to noncombat duty at the Naval Training Station in <a href="/wiki/Bainbridge,_Maryland" class="mw-redirect" title="Bainbridge, Maryland">Bainbridge, Maryland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In June 1945, he was assigned to Special Services in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, and was assigned to a ferry launch unit to bring back damaged ship crews entering <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a> where he was able to play baseball every afternoon in the naval base\'s eight-team league.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansch67_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansch67-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> After being granted emergency leave to see his ailing father in January 1946, he was briefly assigned to the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Navy_Yard" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia Navy Yard">Philadelphia Navy Yard</a> before his honorable discharge from the Navy as a Seaman Second Class in March 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansch67_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansch67-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2007, Musial received the Navy Memorial\'s Lone Sailor Award, which honors Navy veterans who have excelled in civilian life.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Major_leagues_.281946.E2.80.9363.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_leagues_(1946\xe2\x80\x9363)">Major leagues (1946\xe2\x80\x9363)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stan_Musial&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Major leagues (1946\xe2\x80\x9363)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span id="1946.E2.80.9349"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1946\xe2\x80\x9349">1946\xe2\x80\x9349</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stan_Musial&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1946\xe2\x80\x9349">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:27%;&#32;; padding:8px"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"Every time Stan came up they chanted, \'Here comes the man!\'"</div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">\xe2\x80\x94Cardinals traveling secretary Leo Ward relates Dodger fans\' nickname for Musial to sportswriter Bob Broeg<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche75_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche75-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Rejoining the Cardinals under new manager <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Dyer" title="Eddie Dyer">Eddie Dyer</a>, Musial posted a .388 batting average by the middle of May <a href="/wiki/1946_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1946 St. Louis Cardinals season">1946</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> He also became close friends with new teammate <a href="/wiki/Red_Schoendienst" title="Red Schoendienst">Red Schoendienst</a>, who had joined the Cardinals during Musial\'s absence in 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> During the season, Musial (who was under contract to the Cardinals for $13,500 in 1946) was offered a five-year, $125,000 contract, plus a $50,000 bonus, to join the Mexican League.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> He declined the offer, and after manager Dyer spoke to club owner <a href="/wiki/Sam_Breadon" title="Sam Breadon">Sam Breadon</a>, Musial was given a $5,000 raise later in 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was also during the 1946 season that Musial acquired his nickname of <b>Stan the Man</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche75_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche75-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> During the June 23 game against the Dodgers at <a href="/wiki/Ebbets_Field" title="Ebbets Field">Ebbets Field</a>, <i>St. Louis Post-Dispatch</i> sportswriter <a href="/wiki/Bob_Broeg" title="Bob Broeg">Bob Broeg</a> heard Dodger fans chanting whenever Musial came to bat, but could not understand the words.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche75_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche75-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Later that day over dinner, Broeg asked Cardinals traveling secretary Leo Ward if he had understood what the Dodger fans had been chanting.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche75_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche75-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Ward said, "Every time Stan came up they chanted, \'Here comes the man!\'" "\'That man,\' you mean", Broeg said. "No, <i>the</i> man", replied Ward.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche75_68-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche75-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Broeg mentioned this story in his <i>Post-Dispatch</i> column, and Musial was thereafter known as Stan "The Man".<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche75_68-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche75-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stan_Musial.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stan_Musial.png/170px-Stan_Musial.png" width="170" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stan_Musial.png/255px-Stan_Musial.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stan_Musial.png/340px-Stan_Musial.png 2x" data-file-width="682" data-file-height="993" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stan_Musial.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Musial on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Baseball_Digest" title="Baseball Digest">Baseball Digest</a></i>, September 1948</div></div></div>\n<p>In June 1946, Dyer began to use Musial as a <a href="/wiki/First_baseman" title="First baseman">first baseman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The Cardinals finished the season tied with the Dodgers,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> prompting a <a href="/wiki/1946_National_League_tie-breaker_series" title="1946 National League tie-breaker series">three-game playoff</a> for the pennant. Musial\'s Game&#160;1 triple and Game&#160;2 double contributed to the Cardinals\' two-games-to-none series victory.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Facing the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a>, the Cardinals won the <a href="/wiki/1946_World_Series" title="1946 World Series">1946 World Series</a> four games to three,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> as Musial had six hits and four RBI.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> He batted .365 for the season and won his second NL MVP Award, receiving 22 out of a possible 24 first-place votes, finishing ahead of Brooklyn\'s <a href="/wiki/Dixie_Walker" title="Dixie Walker">Dixie Walker</a> (.319 batting average).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Musial began the <a href="/wiki/1947_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1947 St. Louis Cardinals season">1947 season</a> by hitting .146 in April.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> On May 9, team doctor Dr. Robert Hyland confirmed a previous diagnosis of <a href="/wiki/Appendicitis" title="Appendicitis">appendicitis</a>, while discovering that Musial was concurrently suffering from <a href="/wiki/Tonsillitis" title="Tonsillitis">tonsillitis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> He received treatment, but did not have either his appendix or tonsils surgically removed until after the season ended.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Despite his health woes, he finished the year with a batting average of .312.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fully recovered from his ailments, Musial recorded his 1,000th career hit on April 25, <a href="/wiki/1948_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1948 St. Louis Cardinals season">1948</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> After a May 7 <i><a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Globe-Democrat" title="St. Louis Globe-Democrat">St. Louis Globe-Democrat</a></i> article criticized baseball players for appearing in <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_advertising" class="mw-redirect" title="Tobacco advertising">cigarette advertisements</a>, he made a personal decision to never again appear in such ads.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> By June 24, his batting average was .408, prompting Brooklyn pitcher <a href="/wiki/Preacher_Roe" title="Preacher Roe">Preacher Roe</a> to comically announce his new method for retiring Musial: "Walk him on four pitches and <a href="/wiki/Pickoff" title="Pickoff">pick him off</a> <a href="/wiki/First_base" class="mw-redirect" title="First base">first</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Given a mid-season pay raise by new Cardinals owner <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Hannegan" title="Robert E. Hannegan">Robert E. Hannegan</a> for his outstanding performance, Musial hit a home run in the All-Star Game.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>  On September 22, he registered five hits in a game for the fourth time in the season, tying a mark set by <a href="/wiki/Ty_Cobb" title="Ty Cobb">Ty Cobb</a> in 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:27%;&#32;; padding:8px"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"He missed tying for the top in homers by one rained out home run. If it had counted, he would have won the Triple Crown that year...and in addition have been the <i>only</i> player of this century to lead the league in runs, hits, double, triples, and slugging percentage. What a year!"</div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">\xe2\x80\x94Sportswriter Bob Broeg, on Musial\'s 1948 season<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Musial finished the 1948 season leading the major leagues in batting average (.376), hits (230), doubles (46), triples (18), total bases (429), and slugging percentage (.702).<sup id="cite_ref-Gig167_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig167-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Winning the NL batting title by a 43-point margin, with an on-base percentage lead of 27 points and a 138-point slugging percentage margin\xe2\x80\x94the latter being the largest gap since <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Hornsby" title="Rogers Hornsby">Rogers Hornsby</a>\'s 1925 season\xe2\x80\x94Musial became the first player to win three NL MVP awards.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig167_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig167-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> If a home run he hit during a <a href="/wiki/Rainout_(sports)" title="Rainout (sports)">rained out</a> game had been counted in his season totals, he would have won the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Triple_Crown" class="mw-redirect" title="Major League Baseball Triple Crown">Triple Crown</a> by leading the NL in batting average, home runs, and runs batted in.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig167_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig167-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Anticipating life after his baseball career, Musial began the first of several business partnerships with Julius "Biggie" Garagnani in January 1949, opening "Stan Musial &amp; Biggie\'s" restaurant.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> He approached the <a href="/wiki/1949_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1949 St. Louis Cardinals season">1949 season</a> with the intent to try to hit more home runs, stating he had hit 39 the previous season "without trying".<sup id="cite_ref-Gig174_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig174-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> His new focus on hitting for power backfired, as pitchers began using the outside part of the plate to induce him to ground out to the first or <a href="/wiki/Second_base" class="mw-redirect" title="Second base">second baseman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig174_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig174-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Musial soon stopped focusing on hitting home runs and resumed his consistent offensive production by the end of May.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig174_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig174-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> He received his sixth consecutive All-Star player selection and finished the season leading the NL in hits (207) while playing in every game.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> However, the Cardinals, with 96 wins, finished one game behind the Dodgers.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late 1940s, when baseball was slowly becoming <a href="/wiki/Racial_integration" title="Racial integration">integrated</a>, Musial\xe2\x80\x94along with his roommate Red Schoendienst\xe2\x80\x94would be lauded by newcomers such as Dodgers\' pitcher <a href="/wiki/Don_Newcombe" title="Don Newcombe">Don Newcombe</a> for their tolerance. "They never...had the need to sit in the dugout and call a black guy a bunch of names", Newcombe said, "because he was trying to change the game and make it what it should have been in the first place, a game for all people."<sup id="cite_ref-TimesObit_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TimesObit-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1950.E2.80.9354"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1950\xe2\x80\x9354">1950\xe2\x80\x9354</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stan_Musial&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1950\xe2\x80\x9354">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Musial began the 1950s by posting a .350 batting average before participating in the 1950 All-Star Game, where in fan balloting he was the NL\'s number two choice.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig87_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig87-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> He had the longest hitting streak of his career during the <a href="/wiki/1950_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1950 St. Louis Cardinals season">1950 season</a>\xe2\x80\x94a 30-game stretch that ended on July 27.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig87_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig87-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> With the Cardinals falling 14 games out of first place by September, manager Dyer used him at first base and all three outfield positions.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig87_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig87-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> New Cardinals manager <a href="/wiki/Marty_Marion" title="Marty Marion">Marty Marion</a> led the team to a third-place finish in <a href="/wiki/1951_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1951 St. Louis Cardinals season">1951</a>, while Musial was named <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sporting_News" class="mw-redirect" title="The Sporting News">The Sporting News</a></i> Major League Player of the Year.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:27%;&#32;; padding:8px"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"No man has ever been a perfect ballplayer. Stan Musial, however, is the closest to being perfect in the game today.... He plays as hard when his club is away out in front of a game as he does when they\'re just a run or two behind."</div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Ty_Cobb" title="Ty Cobb">Ty Cobb</a>, on Musial in a 1952 <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> magazine article<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche118_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche118-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>National media attention inadvertently turned to Musial a month before the <a href="/wiki/1952_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1952 St. Louis Cardinals season">1952 season</a> began, after <a href="/wiki/Ty_Cobb" title="Ty Cobb">Ty Cobb</a> wrote an article regarding modern baseball players that was published in <a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)"><i>Life</i> magazine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche118_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche118-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Cobb singled out Musial and <a href="/wiki/Phil_Rizzuto" title="Phil Rizzuto">Phil Rizzuto</a> as the only players "who can be mentioned in the same breath with the oldtime greats".<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche118_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche118-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Cobb went on to refer to Musial as "a better player than <a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a> was in his prime."<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche118_101-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche118-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In response, Musial displayed his characteristic modesty, saying, "Cobb is baseball\'s greatest. I don\'t want to contradict him, but I can\'t say that I was ever as good as Joe DiMaggio."<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche118_101-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche118-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The only major league pitching appearance of Musial\'s career occurred as a publicity stunt during the last Cardinals home game of the 1952 season.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig231_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig231-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Manager <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Stanky" title="Eddie Stanky">Eddie Stanky</a> had a reluctant Musial pitch to <a href="/wiki/Frank_Baumholtz" title="Frank Baumholtz">Frank Baumholtz</a>, the runner-up to Musial for the best batting average in the NL that season.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig231_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig231-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> With Baumholtz batting right-handed for the first time in his career, Musial\'s first pitch was hit so hard it ricocheted off the shin of third baseman <a href="/wiki/Solly_Hemus" title="Solly Hemus">Solly Hemus</a> and into the left field corner.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig231_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig231-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The play was ruled an <a href="/wiki/Error_(baseball)" title="Error (baseball)">error</a>, and Musial was embarrassed enough by his complicity in the gimmick to avoid pitching again for the remainder of his career.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig231_102-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig231-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Cardinals franchise was up for sale in early <a href="/wiki/1953_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1953 St. Louis Cardinals season">1953</a>, and Musial and Schoendienst advised their friend and fellow duck-hunter <a href="/wiki/Gussie_Busch" title="Gussie Busch">Gussie Busch</a> to consider buying the team.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig184_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig184-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Busch used the resources of the <a href="/wiki/Anheuser-Busch" title="Anheuser-Busch">Anheuser-Busch</a> company to purchase the Cardinals, keeping Musial in St. Louis by averting the possibility of a move by the team to another city.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig184_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig184-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The 1953 season marked Musial\'s 10th NL All-Star selection, and the 12th consecutive time he finished a major league season with a batting average above .300.<sup id="cite_ref-stats1_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats1-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Musial accomplished another historical feat on May 2, <a href="/wiki/1954_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1954 St. Louis Cardinals season">1954</a>, in a doubleheader in St. Louis against the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Giants_(NL)" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Giants (NL)">New York Giants</a>: he hit three home runs in the first contest, then added two more in the second to become the first major leaguer to hit five home runs in a doubleheader.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig195_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig195-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In addition to his five home runs, he also hit a single in the first game, setting a new record of 21 total bases for a doubleheader.<sup id="cite_ref-Gig195_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gig195-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The only player besides Musial to hit five home runs in a doubleheader is <a href="/wiki/Nate_Colbert" title="Nate Colbert">Nate Colbert</a>, who achieved the feat in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1955.E2.80.9359"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1955\xe2\x80\x9359">1955\xe2\x80\x9359</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stan_Musial&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1955\xe2\x80\x9359">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Musial made his 12th NL All-Star appearance in <a href="/wiki/1955_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1955 St. Louis Cardinals season">1955</a> as a reserve player, when Cincinnati\'s <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kluszewski" title="Ted Kluszewski">Ted Kluszewski</a> outpolled him by 150,000 votes to get on the starting lineup at first base.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Musial entered the game as a pinch hitter in the fourth inning, and played left field as the game entered extra innings.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche137_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche137-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Leading off the bottom of the 12th, he hit a home run to give the NL a 6\xe2\x80\x935 victory.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche137_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche137-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stan_Musial_1957.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Stan_Musial_1957.png/170px-Stan_Musial_1957.png" width="170" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Stan_Musial_1957.png/255px-Stan_Musial_1957.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Stan_Musial_1957.png/340px-Stan_Musial_1957.png 2x" data-file-width="370" data-file-height="438" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stan_Musial_1957.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Musial in 1957</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/1956_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1956 St. Louis Cardinals season">1956 season</a> marked another milestone for Musial, when he broke <a href="/wiki/Mel_Ott" title="Mel Ott">Mel Ott</a>\'s NL record for extra-base hits on August 12.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Earlier that season, Cardinals general manager <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lane" title="Frank Lane">"Trader Frank" Lane</a> began negotiations to trade him for Philadelphia pitcher <a href="/wiki/Robin_Roberts_(baseball)" title="Robin Roberts (baseball)">Robin Roberts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> When Cardinals owner Gussie Busch learned of the possible move, he made it clear that Musial was not available for any trade.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Instead, Lane dealt Musial\'s close friend Schoendienst to the New York Giants; an upset Musial made no immediate comment to the press.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On June 11, <a href="/wiki/1957_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1957 St. Louis Cardinals season">1957</a>, Musial tied the NL record for consecutive games played with his 822nd, a streak that began on the last day of the 1951 season.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Despite <a href="/wiki/Ballot_stuffing" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballot stuffing">ballot stuffing</a> by Cincinnati Reds fans, he was selected and played in the All-Star Game held at Sportsman\'s Park.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> When he overextended his swing while batting during a game on August 23, Musial fractured a bone in his left shoulder socket and tore muscles over his <a href="/wiki/Clavicle" title="Clavicle">collarbone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche151_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche151-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> He was unable to play again until September 8, ending his <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_consecutive_games_played_streaks" title="Major League Baseball consecutive games played streaks">consecutive games-played streak</a> at 895.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche151_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche151-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> He finished 1957 as <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s "<a href="/wiki/Sportsman_of_the_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="Sportsman of the Year">Sportsman of the Year</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:27%;&#32;; padding:8px"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"Line drive! Into left field! Hit number three thousand! A run has scored! Musial around first, on his way to second with a double. Holy Cow! He came through!"</div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Harry_Caray" title="Harry Caray">Harry Caray</a>\'s radio play-by-play call of Musial\'s 3,000th major league hit<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Musial signed one of the first $100,000 contracts in NL history on January 29, 1958. (According to Baseball Almanac, Hank Greenberg was the first with Pittsburgh in 1947.)<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> He quickly demonstrated a return on the investment by sharing with Willie Mays the inaugural (and for the only time in Musial\'s career) <a href="/wiki/MLB_Player_of_the_Month" class="mw-redirect" title="MLB Player of the Month">NL Player of the Month</a> in May (no such award was given in April until 1969) batting .374, with 4 HR, and 16 RBI. Also that month, as he was approaching the 3,000-hit milestone in his major league career, he expressed a desire to record the hit in St. Louis.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> He ultimately reached the mark with a pinch-hit, sixth inning RBI double at Chicago\'s <a href="/wiki/Wrigley_Field" title="Wrigley Field">Wrigley Field</a> on May 13.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The eighth major league player to reach 3,000 hits, and the first to reach the milestone with an extra-base hit, Musial was greeted at <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Union_Station" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis Union Station">St. Louis Union Station</a> that evening by roughly 1,000&#160;fans.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Finishing the season in sixth place, the Cardinals embarked on an exhibition tour of Japan, winning 14 of 16&#160;games against top players from the <a href="/wiki/Central_League" title="Central League">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_League" title="Pacific League">Pacific</a> Japanese Leagues.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Taking a new approach to preparation for the <a href="/wiki/1959_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1959 St. Louis Cardinals season">1959 season</a>, Musial was given permission to report late to spring training so that he might conserve his energy for the duration of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Musial, at 6 feet (180&#160;cm) tall, had maintained a weight of around 175 pounds (79&#160;kg) throughout his career. He reported to spring training approximately 10 pounds (4.5&#160;kg) overweight and in substandard physical condition.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lansche166_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche166-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> He began the season with one hit in 15 at-bats.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche166_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche166-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Despite his early offensive struggles, he single-handedly spoiled potential no-hitters on April 16 and 19.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche166_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche166-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> A game-winning home run on May 7 made him the first major league player ever with 400 home runs and 3,000 hits.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche166_128-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche166-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> As he continued to hit at a relatively low pace, his playing time was limited by Cardinals manager Solly Hemus at various points during the season.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Seeking more revenue for the players\' pension fund, Major League Baseball held two All-Star games in a season for the first time through 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche168_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche168-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Musial made his 16th All-Star appearance (16th season)<sup id="cite_ref-sportsdatallc.com_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sportsdatallc.com-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and pinch-hit in both contests, flying out in the July 7 game and drawing a walk in the August 3 game.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche168_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche168-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> He finished the season with 115 regular game appearances, a .255 batting average, 37 runs, and a slugging percentage of .428.<sup id="cite_ref-stats1_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats1-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1959 season, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> approached Musial about supporting Kennedy\'s <a href="/wiki/1960_elections" class="mw-redirect" title="1960 elections">campaign for President</a>, citing their close ages. Musial campaigned for Kennedy later that year and became a supporter of the Democrat.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On June 30, 1959, Musial was the batter in one of the oddest plays in baseball history. In a game between the Cardinals and <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a>, he was at the plate with a count of 3\xe2\x80\x931. <a href="/wiki/Bob_Anderson_(baseball)" title="Bob Anderson (baseball)">Bob Anderson\'s</a> next pitch was errant, evading catcher <a href="/wiki/Sammy_Taylor_(baseball)" title="Sammy Taylor (baseball)">Sammy Taylor</a> and rolling all the way to the backstop. Umpire <a href="/wiki/Vic_Delmore" title="Vic Delmore">Vic Delmore</a> called ball four, even as Anderson and Taylor contended that Musial had <a href="/wiki/Foul_tip" title="Foul tip">foul tipped</a> the ball. Because the ball was still in play and Delmore was embroiled in an argument with the catcher and pitcher, Musial kept running in attempt to make second base. Seeing that Musial was trying for second, <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Dark" title="Alvin Dark">Alvin Dark</a> ran to the backstop to retrieve the ball. The ball wound up in the hands of field announcer <a href="/wiki/Pat_Pieper" title="Pat Pieper">Pat Pieper</a>, but Dark ended up getting it back anyway. Absentmindedly, however, Delmore pulled out a new ball and gave it to Taylor. Anderson finally noticed that Musial was trying for second, took the new ball, and threw it to second baseman <a href="/wiki/Tony_Taylor_(baseball)" title="Tony Taylor (baseball)">Tony Taylor</a>. Anderson\'s throw flew over Taylor\'s head into the outfield. Dark, at the same time that Anderson threw the new ball, threw the original ball to shortstop <a href="/wiki/Ernie_Banks" title="Ernie Banks">Ernie Banks</a>. Musial did not see Dark\'s throw and only noticed Anderson\'s ball fly over the second baseman\'s head, so he tried to go to third base. On his way there, he was tagged by Banks, and after a delay he was ruled out.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1960.E2.80.9363"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1960\xe2\x80\x9363">1960\xe2\x80\x9363</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stan_Musial&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1960\xe2\x80\x9363">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Based on his 1959 performance, Musial accepted a pay cut in <a href="/wiki/1960_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1960 St. Louis Cardinals season">1960</a> from his previous $100,000 salary to $80,000.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> He was eager to prove that his mediocre performance was the result of improper physical conditioning, and he enlisted the help of Walter Eberhardt, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Louis_University" title="Saint Louis University">Saint Louis University</a>\'s director of physical education.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> In June 1960, newspaper articles began speculating that Musial would soon retire, yet he finished the season with a .275 batting average.<sup id="cite_ref-stats1_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats1-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> He addressed the speculation in September, confirming that he would play again in <a href="/wiki/1961_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1961 St. Louis Cardinals season">1961</a>. His .288 batting average that season reaffirmed his decision.<sup id="cite_ref-stats1_47-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats1-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In 1962, Musial posted a .330 batting average, good for third in the batting race, with 19 homers and 82&#160;RBI. As a pinch-hitter, he had 14 base hits in 19 at-bats (.737).<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Along the way, he established new NL career marks for hits, RBI, and runs scored. That same year on July 8, the 41-year-old Musial became the oldest player ever to hit three home runs in one game.\n</p>\n<table style="width: 100px; font-size: 90%; float:right; border: 2px solid #aaa; margin: 0em 0em 0em 1em;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center;"><a href="/wiki/File:CardsRetired6.PNG" class="image"><img alt="CardsRetired6.PNG" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/CardsRetired6.PNG/95px-CardsRetired6.PNG" width="95" height="77" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/CardsRetired6.PNG 1.5x" data-file-width="117" data-file-height="95" /></a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">Stan Musial\'s number 6 was <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_retired_numbers" title="List of Major League Baseball retired numbers">retired</a> by the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals#Retired_numbers" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Cardinals</a> in 1963.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The Cardinals began <a href="/wiki/1963_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1963 St. Louis Cardinals season">1963</a> by winning 10 of their first 15 games, as Musial posted a .237 batting average.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> He set a new major league record for career extra-base hits on May 8 and improved his batting average to .277 by the end of the month.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche191_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche191-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Making his 20th All-Star appearance<sup id="cite_ref-sportsdatallc.com_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sportsdatallc.com-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and 24th All-Star Game appearance on July 9, 1963, he pinch-hit in the fifth inning.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche191_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche191-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Asked by general manager <a href="/wiki/Bing_Devine" title="Bing Devine">Bing Devine</a> on July 26 what his plans were, Musial said that he would retire at season\'s end.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> He waited until the Cardinals team picnic on August 12 to publicly announce his decision, hopeful he could retire on a winning note.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Musial became a grandfather for the first time in the early hours of September 10; later that day, he hit a home run in his first at-bat.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche193_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche193-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> After sweeping a doubleheader on September 15, the Cardinals had won 19 of their last 20 games, and were one game behind the Los Angeles Dodgers.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche193_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche193-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The Dodgers then swept the Cardinals in a three-game series in St. Louis and clinched the NL pennant on September 25.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Musial\'s last game, on September 29, 1963, was preceded by an hour-long retirement ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche196_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche196-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>  Speakers at the event included baseball commissioner <a href="/wiki/Ford_Frick" title="Ford Frick">Ford Frick</a>, Cardinals broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Harry_Caray" title="Harry Caray">Harry Caray</a>, and Cardinals owner Gussie Busch, who announced that Musial\'s uniform number "6" would be <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_retired_numbers" title="List of Major League Baseball retired numbers">retired</a> by the team.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche196_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche196-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> During the game, Musial recorded a single in the fourth inning, then hit a single to right field that scored teammate <a href="/wiki/Curt_Flood" title="Curt Flood">Curt Flood</a> in the sixth.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche197_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche197-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Cardinals manager <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Keane" title="Johnny Keane">Johnny Keane</a> brought in <a href="/wiki/Gary_Kolb" title="Gary Kolb">Gary Kolb</a> as a pinch-runner for Musial, bringing his major league career to an end.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche197_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche197-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Just as he had recorded two base hits in his major league debut, Musial finished his last game with two hits, as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Lansche197_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lansche197-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Musial finished with the all-time National League hits record and second to only <a href="/wiki/Ty_Cobb" title="Ty Cobb">Ty Cobb</a> on the all-time Major League list. Musial\'s last career hit was out of reach of  Cincinnati Reds second baseman <a href="/wiki/Pete_Rose" title="Pete Rose">Pete Rose</a>, who would go on to break Cobb\'s record to become baseball\'s all-time hit king.\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:27%;&#32;; padding:8px"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"All Musial represents is more than two decades of sustained excellence and complete decency as a human being."</div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned"
